Franklin">Gene Franklin</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; background: transparent; text-align: left; padding-left: 5px; padding-top: 3px"><a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">Astronauts</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">The Mercury Seven</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">Gemini/Apollo astronauts</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Corps" title="NASA Astronaut Corps">NASA Astronaut Corps</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:U.S._space_program_sidebar" title="Template:U.S. space program sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:U.S._space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:U.S. space program sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:U.S._space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Project Gemini</b> was <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s second <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a> program. Conducted between projects <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Mercury</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_Apollo" title="Project Apollo">Apollo</a>, Gemini started in 1961 and concluded in 1966. The Gemini spacecraft carried a two-astronaut crew. Ten Gemini crews flew <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO) missions during 1965 and 1966, putting the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in the lead during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.\n</p><p>Gemini\'s objective was the development of space travel techniques to support the Apollo mission to <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">land astronauts on the Moon</a>. It performed missions long enough for a trip to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and back, perfected working outside the spacecraft with <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extra-vehicular activity</a> (EVA), and pioneered the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_maneuver" title="Orbital maneuver">orbital maneuvers</a> necessary to achieve <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">docking</a>. With these new techniques proven by Gemini, Apollo could pursue its prime mission without doing these fundamental exploratory operations.\n</p><p>All Gemini flights were launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Launch_Complex_19" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Launch Complex 19">Launch Complex 19</a> (LC-19) at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Kennedy Air Force Station</a> in Florida. Their <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> was the Gemini&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Titan II</a>, a modified <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">Intercontinental Ballistic Missile</a> (ICBM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> Gemini was the first program to use the newly built <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">Mission Control Center</a> at the Houston <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Manned Spacecraft Center</a> for <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller#NASA\'s_Flight_Controllers" title="Flight controller">flight control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[Note 2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The astronaut corps that supported Project Gemini included the "<a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">Mercury Seven</a>", "<a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_2" title="NASA Astronaut Group 2">The New Nine</a>", and the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_3" title="NASA Astronaut Group 3">1963 astronaut class</a>. During the program, three astronauts died in air crashes during training, including the prime crew for Gemini 9. This mission was flown by the backup crew, the only time that has happened in NASA\'s history to date.\n</p><p>Gemini was robust enough that the United States Air Force planned to use it for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_Orbital_Laboratory" title="Manned Orbital Laboratory">Manned Orbital Laboratory</a> (MOL) program, which was later canceled. Gemini\'s chief designer, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Chamberlin" title="Jim Chamberlin">Jim Chamberlin</a>, also made detailed plans for <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Cislunar_space" title="Outer space">cislunar</a> and lunar landing missions in late 1961. He believed that Gemini spacecraft could fly in lunar operations before Project Apollo, and cost less. NASA\'s administration did not approve those plans. In 1969, McDonnell-Douglas proposed a "<a href="/wiki/Big_Gemini" title="Big Gemini">Big Gemini</a>" that could have been used to shuttle up to 12 astronauts to the planned space stations in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Project" title="Apollo Applications Project">Apollo Applications Project</a> (AAP). The only AAP project funded was <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> &#8211; which used existing spacecraft and hardware &#8211; thereby eliminating the need for Big Gemini.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Pronunciation"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Pronunciation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Program_origins_and_objectives"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Program origins and objectives</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Team"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Team</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Reentry module</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Adapter_module"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span module</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Launch_vehicle"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Launch <span class="toctext">Program cost</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Current_location_of_hardware"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Current location of hardware</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Spacecraft_2"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Spacecraft</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Trainers"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Trainers</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Proposed_extensions_and_applications"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Proposed extensions and applications</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Advanced_Gemini"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Advanced Gemini</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Big_Gemini"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Big Gemini</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Military_applications"><span class="tocnumber">11.3</span> <span class="toctext">Military applications</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#In_media"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">In media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Books"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Books</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Articles"><span class="tocnumber">14.4</span> <span class="toctext">Articles</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronunciation">Pronunciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Pronunciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The constellation for which the project was named is commonly pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="/d&#658;/: \'j\' in \'jam\'">d&#658;</span><span title="/&#603;/: \'e\' in \'dress\'">&#603;</span><span title="\'m\' in \'my\'">m</span><span title="/&#618;/: \'i\' in \'kit\'">&#618;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/a&#618;/: \'i\' in \'tide\'">a&#618;</span></span>/</a></span></span>, the last syllable rhyming with <i>eye</i>. However, staff of the Manned Spacecraft Center, including the astronauts, tended to pronounce the name <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="/d&#658;/: \'j\' in \'jam\'">d&#658;</span><span title="/&#603;/: \'e\' in \'dress\'">&#603;</span><span title="\'m\' in \'my\'">m</span><span title="/&#618;/: \'i\' in \'kit\'">&#618;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/i/: \'y\' in \'happy\'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>, rhyming with <i>knee</i>. NASA\'s public affairs office issued a statement in 1965 declaring "Jeh-mih-nee" to be the "official" pronunciation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTFirstMan_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTFirstMan-4">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">Gus Grissom</a>, acting as Houston <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capsule_communicator" title="Capsule communicator">capsule communicator</a> when <a href="/wiki/Ed_White_(astronaut)" title="Ed White (astronaut)">Ed White</a> performed his spacewalk on <a href="/wiki/Gemini_4" title="Gemini 4">Gemini 4</a>, is heard on flight recordings pronouncing the spacecraft\'s call sign "Jeh-mih-nee 4", and the NASA pronunciation is used in the movie <i><a href="/wiki/First_Man_(film)" title="First Man (film)">First Man</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTFirstMan_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTFirstMan-4">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_origins_and_objectives">Program origins and objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Program origins and objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> was conceived in early 1960 as a three-man spacecraft to follow <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jim_Chamberlin" title="Jim Chamberlin">Jim Chamberlin</a>, the head of engineering at the <a href="/wiki/Space_Task_Group" title="Space Task Group">Space Task Group</a> (STG), was assigned in February 1961 to start working on a bridge program between Mercury and Apollo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99-5">[3]</a></sup> He presented two initial versions of a two-man spacecraft, then designated Mercury Mark II, at a NASA retreat at Wallops Island in March 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99-5">[3]</a></sup> Scale models were shown in July 1961 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell Aircraft Corporation</a>\'s offices in St. Louis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99-5">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Apollo was chartered to land men on the Moon by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_John_F._Kennedy" title="President John F. Kennedy">President John F. Kennedy</a> on May 25, 1961, it became evident to NASA officials that a follow-on to the Mercury program was required to develop certain spaceflight capabilities in support of Apollo.  NASA approved the two-man program rechristened Project Gemini (Latin for "twins"), in reference to <a href="/wiki/Gemini_(constellation)" title="Gemini (constellation)">the third constellation of the Zodiac</a> with its twin stars <a href="/wiki/Castor_(star)" title="Castor (star)">Castor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollux_(star)" title="Pollux (star)">Pollux</a>, on December 7, 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGainor200193,_97&#8211;99-5">[3]</a></sup> McDonnell Aircraft was contracted to build it on December 22, 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerGrimwood1977XV,_75_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerGrimwood1977XV,_75-6">[4]</a></sup> The program was publicly announced on January 3, 1962, with these major objectives:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoff2013_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoff2013-7">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>To demonstrate endurance of humans and equipment in spaceflight for extended periods, at least eight days required for a Moon landing, to a maximum of two weeks</li>\n<li>To effect <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">rendezvous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">docking</a> with another vehicle, and to maneuver the combined spacecraft using the propulsion system of the target vehicle</li>\n<li>To demonstrate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">Extra-Vehicular Activity</a> (EVA), or space-"walks" outside the protection of the spacecraft, and to evaluate the astronauts\' ability to perform tasks there</li>\n<li>To perfect techniques of atmospheric reentry and touchdown at a pre-selected location on land<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[Note 3]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Team">Team</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Team">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Canadian Jim Chamberlin designed the Gemini capsule, which carried a crew of two. He was previously the chief <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerodynamicist" title="Aerodynamicist">aerodynamicist</a> on <a href="/wiki/Avro_Canada" title="Avro Canada">Avro Canada</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avro_Arrow" title="Avro Arrow">Avro Arrow</a> fighter interceptor program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayCox198933&#8211;34_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayCox198933&#8211;34-9">[6]</a></sup> Chamberlin joined NASA along with 25 senior Avro engineers after cancellation of the Arrow program, and became head of the U.S. Space Task Group\'s engineering division in charge of Gemini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayCox198933&#8211;34_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayCox198933&#8211;34-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReguly19657_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReguly19657-10">[7]</a></sup> The prime contractor was McDonnell Aircraft Corporation, which was also the prime contractor for the <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a> capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerGrimwood197775_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerGrimwood197775-11">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">Astronaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">Gus Grissom</a> was heavily involved in the development and design of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_spacecraft" title="Gemini spacecraft">Gemini spacecraft</a>. What other Mercury astronauts dubbed "Gusmobile" was so designed around Grissom\'s 5\'6" body that, when NASA discovered in 1963 that 14 of 16 astronauts would not fit in the spacecraft, the interior had to be redesigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgle1998_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgle1998-12">[9]</a></sup> Grissom wrote in his posthumous 1968 book <i>Gemini!</i> that the realization of <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a>\'s end and the unlikelihood of his having another flight in that program prompted him to focus all of his efforts on the upcoming Gemini program.\n</p><p>The Gemini program was managed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Manned Spacecraft Center</a>, located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>, under direction of the Office of Manned Space Flight, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> Headquarters, Washington, D.C. Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_E._Mueller" title="George E. Mueller">George E. Mueller</a>, Associate Administrator of NASA for Manned Space Flight, served as acting director of the Gemini program. William C. Schneider, Deputy Director of Manned Space Flight for Mission Operations, served as mission director on all Gemini flights beginning with Gemini 6A.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guenter_Wendt" title="Guenter Wendt">Guenter Wendt</a> was a McDonnell engineer who supervised launch preparations for both the Mercury and Gemini programs and would go on to do the same when the Apollo program launched crews. His team was responsible for completion of the complex pad close-out procedures just prior to spacecraft launch, and he was the last person the astronauts would see prior to closing the hatch. The astronauts appreciated his taking absolute authority over, and responsibility for, the condition of the spacecraft and developed a good-humored rapport with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarmerHamblin200451&#8211;54_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarmerHamblin200451&#8211;54-13">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">Spacecraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spacecraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_spacecraft.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2147" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Gemini_spacecraft.jpg/220px-Gemini_spacecraft.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Gemini_spacecraft.jpg/330px-Gemini_spacecraft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Gemini_spacecraft.jpg/440px-Gemini_spacecraft.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_spacecraft.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cutaway illustration of the Gemini spacecraft</div></div></div>\n<p>NASA selected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell Aircraft</a>, which had been the prime contractor for the <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a> capsule, in 1961 to build the Gemini capsule, the first of which was delivered in 1963. The spacecraft was 18&#160;feet 5&#160;inches (5.61&#160;m) long and 10 feet (3.0&#160;m) wide, with a launch weight varying from 7,100 to 8,350 pounds (3,220 to 3,790&#160;kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGatland197642_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGatland197642-14">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Gemini crew capsule (referred to as the Reentry Module) was essentially an enlarged version of the Mercury capsule. Unlike Mercury, the <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">retrorockets</a>, electrical power, propulsion systems, oxygen, and water were located in a detachable Adapter Module behind the Reentry Module. A major design improvement in Gemini was to locate all internal spacecraft systems in modular components, which could be independently tested and replaced when necessary, without removing or disturbing other already tested components.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_heat_shield.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="4864" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Gemini_heat_shield.jpg/220px-Gemini_heat_shield.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Gemini_heat_shield.jpg/330px-Gemini_heat_shield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Gemini_heat_shield.jpg/440px-Gemini_heat_shield.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_heat_shield.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unablated Gemini heat shield</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg/220px-Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg/330px-Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg/440px-Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_Spacecraft_Heat_Shield.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ablated Gemini heat shield</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reentry_module">Reentry module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reentry module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Many components in the capsule itself were reachable through their own small access doors. Unlike Mercury, Gemini used completely solid-state electronics, and its modular design made it easy to repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDryden1964362_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDryden1964362-15">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gemini\'s emergency <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">launch escape system</a> did not use an escape tower powered by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">solid-fuel rocket</a>, but instead used aircraft-style <a href="/wiki/Ejection_seat" title="Ejection seat">ejection seats</a>. The tower was heavy and complicated, and NASA engineers reasoned that they could do away with it as the Titan II\'s <a href="/wiki/Hypergolic_propellant" title="Hypergolic propellant">hypergolic propellants</a> would burn immediately on contact. A Titan II booster explosion had a smaller blast effect and flame than on the cryogenically fueled Atlas and Saturn. <a href="/wiki/Ejection_seat" title="Ejection seat">Ejection seats</a> were sufficient to separate the astronauts from a malfunctioning launch vehicle. At higher altitudes, where the ejection seats could not be used, the astronauts would return inside the spacecraft, which would separate from the launch vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDryden1965364_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDryden1965364-16">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main proponent of using ejection seats was <a href="/wiki/Jim_Chamberlin" title="Jim Chamberlin">James Chamberlin</a>, head of the engineering division of NASA\'s Space Force Task Group. Chamberlin had never liked the Mercury escape tower and wished to use a simpler alternative that would also reduce weight. He reviewed several films of Atlas and Titan II ICBM failures, which he used to estimate the approximate size of a fireball produced by an exploding launch vehicle and from this he gauged that the Titan II would produce a much smaller explosion, thus the spacecraft could get away with ejection seats.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maxime_Faget" title="Maxime Faget">Maxime Faget</a>, the designer of the Mercury LES, was on the other hand less-than-enthusiastic about this setup. Aside from the possibility of the ejection seats seriously injuring the astronauts, they would also only be usable for about 40 seconds after liftoff, by which point the booster would be attaining Mach 1 speed and ejection would no longer be possible. He was also concerned about the astronauts being launched through the Titan\'s exhaust plume if they ejected in-flight and later added that "The best thing about Gemini was that they never had to make an escape."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gemini was the first astronaut-carrying spacecraft to include an onboard computer, the <a href="/wiki/Gemini_Guidance_Computer" title="Gemini Guidance Computer">Gemini Guidance Computer</a>, to facilitate management and control of mission maneuvers. This computer, sometimes called the Gemini Spacecraft On-Board Computer (OBC), was very similar to the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Launch_Vehicle_Digital_Computer" title="Saturn Launch Vehicle Digital Computer">Saturn Launch Vehicle Digital Computer</a>. The Gemini Guidance Computer weighed 58.98 pounds (26.75&#160;kg). Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_memory" title="Core memory">core memory</a> had 4096 <a href="/wiki/Memory_address" title="Memory address">addresses</a>, each containing a 39-bit <a href="/wiki/Word_(computer_architecture)" title="Word (computer architecture)">word</a> composed of three 13-bit "syllables". All numeric data was <a href="/wiki/26-bit" title="26-bit">26-bit</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two%27s-complement" title="Two\'s-complement">two\'s-complement</a> integers (sometimes used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed-point_number" title="Fixed-point number">fixed-point numbers</a>), either stored in the first two syllables of a word or in the <a href="/wiki/Accumulator_(computing)" title="Accumulator (computing)">accumulator</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Instruction_set" title="Instruction set">Instructions</a> (always with a 4-bit <a href="/wiki/Opcode" title="Opcode">opcode</a> and 9 bits of operand) could go in any syllable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETomayko198810&#8211;19_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETomayko198810&#8211;19-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurkey2012_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurkey2012-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike Mercury, Gemini used in-flight <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Attitude_indicator" title="Attitude indicator">artificial horizon</a>, similar to those used in the aviation industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETomayko198810&#8211;19_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETomayko198810&#8211;19-18">[15]</a></sup> Astronauts had no control over Mercury\'s flight path, and computers flew most of Apollo missions. Gemini crew had full manual control with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Control_stick" title="Control stick">control sticks</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaw,_pitch,_and_roll" title="Yaw, pitch, and roll">yaw, pitch, and roll</a> and forward or backward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgle1998_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgle1998-12">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_paraglider.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="378" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Gemini_paraglider.JPG/220px-Gemini_paraglider.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Gemini_paraglider.JPG/330px-Gemini_paraglider.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/Gemini_paraglider.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_paraglider.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gemini paraglider during tests at <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a> in August 1964.</div></div></div>\n<p>The original intention for Gemini was to land on solid ground instead of at sea, using a <a href="/wiki/Rogallo_wing" title="Rogallo wing">Rogallo wing</a> rather than a parachute, with the crew seated upright controlling the forward motion of the craft. To facilitate this, the airfoil did not attach just to the nose of the craft, but to an additional attachment point for balance near the heat shield. This cord was covered by a strip of metal which ran between the twin hatches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> This design was ultimately dropped, and parachutes were used to make a sea landing as in Mercury. The capsule was suspended at an angle closer to horizontal, so that a side of the heat shield contacted the water first. This eliminated the need for the landing bag cushion used in the Mercury capsule.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adapter_module">Adapter module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Adapter module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The adapter module in turn was separated into a Retro module and an Equipment module.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Retro_module">Retro module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Retro module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Retro module contained four solid-fuel TE-M-385 Star-13E retrorockets, each spherical in shape except for its rocket nozzle, which were structurally attached to two beams that reached across the diameter of the retro module, crossing at right angles in the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> Re-entry began with the retrorockets firing one at a time. Abort procedures at certain periods during lift-off would cause them to fire at the same time, thrusting the Descent module away from the Titan rocket.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment_module">Equipment module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Equipment module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Gemini was equipped with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbit_attitude_and_maneuvering_system" title="Orbit attitude and maneuvering system">Orbit Attitude and Maneuvering System</a> (OAMS), containing sixteen thrusters for <a href="/wiki/Translation_(geometry)" title="Translation (geometry)">translation</a> control in all three perpendicular axes (forward/backward, left/right, up/down), in addition to <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> (pitch, yaw, and roll angle orientation) as in Mercury. Translation control allowed changing <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">orbital inclination</a> and altitude, necessary to perform <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> with other craft, and <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">docking</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Agena_target_vehicle" title="Agena target vehicle">Agena Target Vehicle</a> (ATV), with its own rocket engine which could be used to perform greater orbit changes.\n</p><p>Early short-duration missions had their electrical power supplied by batteries; later endurance missions used the first <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a> in manned spacecraft.\n</p><p>Gemini was in some regards more advanced than Apollo because the latter program began almost a year earlier. It became known as a "pilot\'s spacecraft" due to its assortment of jet fighter-like features, in no small part due to Gus Grissom\'s influence over the design, and it was at this point where the American manned space program clearly began showing its superiority over that of the Soviet Union with long duration flight, rendezvous, and extravehicular capability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[Note 4]</a></sup> The Soviet Union during this period was developing the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> spacecraft intended to take cosmonauts to the Moon, but political and technical problems began to get in the way, leading to the ultimate end of their manned lunar program.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_vehicle">Launch vehicle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Launch vehicle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Titan II GLV</a></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_II" title="Titan II">Titan II</a> had debuted in 1962 as the Air Force\'s second-generation ICBM to replace the Atlas. By using hypergolic fuels, it could be stored for long periods of time and be easily readied for launch in addition to being a simpler design with fewer components, the only caveat being that the propellant mix (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_tetroxide" title="Nitrogen tetroxide">nitrogen tetroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>) was extremely toxic compared to the Atlas\'s liquid oxygen/RP-1. However, the Titan had considerable difficulty being man-rated due to early problems with <a href="/wiki/Pogo_oscillation" title="Pogo oscillation">pogo oscillation</a>. The launch vehicle used a radio guidance system that was unique to launches from Cape Kennedy.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronauts">Astronauts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Astronauts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1548" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg/220px-Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg/330px-Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg/440px-Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astronauts_White_and_McDivitt_Inside_Gemini_IV_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2002-000031.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronauts White and McDivitt inside the Gemini 4 spacecraft, 1965</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2088" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg/220px-Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg/330px-Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg/440px-Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini8.1966.ws.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gemini 8 prime crew and other astronauts at prelaunch breakfast, 1966</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Deke_Slayton" title="Deke Slayton">Deke Slayton</a>, as director of flight crew operations, had primary responsibility for assigning crews for the Gemini program. Each flight had a primary crew and backup crew, and the backup crew would rotate to primary crew status three flights later. Slayton intended for first choice of mission commands to be given to the four remaining active astronauts of the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">Mercury Seven</a>: <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a>, Grissom, Cooper, and Schirra. (<a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> had retired from NASA in January 1964 and <a href="/wiki/Scott_Carpenter" title="Scott Carpenter">Scott Carpenter</a>, who was blamed by some in NASA management for the problematic reentry of <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_7" title="Mercury-Atlas 7">Aurora 7</a></i>, was on leave to participate in the Navy\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEALAB_(United_States_Navy)" title="SEALAB (United States Navy)">SEALAB</a> project and was grounded from flight in July 1964 due to an arm injury sustained in a motorbike accident. Slayton himself continued to be grounded due to a heart problem.)\n</p><p>Titles used for the left-hand (command) and right-hand seat crew positions were taken from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force_aeronautical_rating" title="U.S. Air Force aeronautical rating">U.S. Air Force pilot ratings</a>, <i>Command Pilot</i> and <i>Pilot</i>. Sixteen astronauts flew on 10 manned Gemini missions:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2198" data-file-width="2819" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg/220px-Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg/330px-Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg/440px-Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_4_Astronauts_Meet_Yuri_Gagarin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> shakes hand with Gemini 4 astronauts, 1965</div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Group\n</th>\n<th>Astronaut\n</th>\n<th>Service\n</th>\n<th>Mission, crew position\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="3"><a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">Astronaut Group 1</a><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a> veterans)\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Cooper" title="Gordon Cooper">Gordon Cooper</a>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2">USAF\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_5" title="Gemini 5">Gemini 5</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">Virgil "Gus" Grissom</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_3" title="Gemini 3">Gemini 3</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Wally_Schirra" title="Wally Schirra">Walter M. Schirra</a>\n</td>\n<td>USN\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_6A" title="Gemini 6A">Gemini 6A</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="12"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Group_2" title="Astronaut Group 2">Astronaut Group 2</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>\n</td>\n<td>Civilian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[Note 5]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_8" title="Gemini 8">Gemini 8</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Frank_Borman" title="Frank Borman">Frank Borman</a>\n</td>\n<td>USAF\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_7" title="Gemini 7">Gemini 7</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Pete_Conrad" title="Pete Conrad">Charles "Pete" Conrad</a>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2">USN\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_5" title="Gemini 5">Gemini 5</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_11" title="Gemini 11">Gemini 11</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2">USN\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_7" title="Gemini 7">Gemini 7</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_12" title="Gemini 12">Gemini 12</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/James_McDivitt" title="James McDivitt">James McDivitt</a>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="4">USAF\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_4" title="Gemini 4">Gemini 4</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Thomas_P._Stafford" title="Thomas P. Stafford">Thomas P. Stafford</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_6A" title="Gemini 6A">Gemini 6A</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_9" title="Gemini 9">Gemini 9</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ed_White_(astronaut)" title="Ed White (astronaut)">Ed White</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_4" title="Gemini 4">Gemini 4</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">John Young</a>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2">USN\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_3" title="Gemini 3">Gemini 3</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_10" title="Gemini 10">Gemini 10</a> Command Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="5"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Group_3" title="Astronaut Group 3">Astronaut Group 3</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Edwin "Buzz" Aldrin</a>\n</td>\n<td>USAF\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_12" title="Gemini 12">Gemini 12</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gene_Cernan" title="Gene Cernan">Eugene Cernan</a>\n</td>\n<td>USN\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_9" title="Gemini 9">Gemini 9</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a>\n</td>\n<td>USAF\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_10" title="Gemini 10">Gemini 10</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Gordon_Jr." title="Richard F. Gordon Jr.">Richard F. Gordon</a>\n</td>\n<td>USN\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_11" title="Gemini 11">Gemini 11</a> Pilot\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/David_Scott" title="David Scott">David Scott</a>\n</td>\n<td>USAF\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gemini_8" title="Gemini 8">Gemini 8</a> Pilot\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_selection">Crew selection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Crew selection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In late 1963, Slayton selected Shepard and Stafford for Gemini 3, McDivitt and White for Gemini 4, and Schirra and Young for Gemini 5 (which was to be the first Agena rendezvous mission). The backup crew for Gemini 3 was Grissom and Borman, who were also slated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_6" title="Gemini 6">Gemini 6</a>, to be the first long-duration mission. Finally Conrad and Lovell were assigned as the backup crew for <a href="/wiki/Gemini_4" title="Gemini 4">Gemini 4</a>.\n</p><p>Delays in the production of the Agena Target Vehicle caused the first rearrangement of the crew rotation. The Schirra and Young mission was bumped to Gemini 6 and they became the backup crew for Shepard and Stafford. Grissom and Borman then had their long-duration mission assigned to Gemini 5.\n</p><p>The second rearrangement occurred when Shepard developed <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9ni%C3%A8re%27s_disease" title="M&#233;ni&#232;re\'s disease">M&#233;ni&#232;re\'s disease</a>, an inner ear problem. Grissom was then moved to command Gemini 3. Slayton felt that Young was a better personality match with Grissom and switched Stafford and Young. Finally, Slayton tapped Cooper to command the long-duration Gemini 5. Again for reasons of compatibility, he moved Conrad from backup commander of Gemini 4 to pilot of Gemini 5, and Borman to backup command of Gemini 4. Finally he assigned Armstrong and <a href="/wiki/Elliot_See" title="Elliot See">Elliot See</a> to be the backup crew for Gemini 5.\nThe third rearrangement of crew assignment occurred when Slayton felt that See wasn\'t up to the physical demands of EVA on Gemini 8. He reassigned See to be the prime commander of Gemini 9 and put Scott as pilot of Gemini 8 and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bassett" title="Charles Bassett">Charles Bassett</a> as the pilot of Gemini 9.\n</p><p>The fourth and final rearrangement of the Gemini crew assignment occurred after the deaths of See and Bassett when their trainer jet crashed, coincidentally into a McDonnell building which held their Gemini 9 capsule in St. Louis. The backup crew of Stafford and Cernan was then moved up to the new prime crew of the re-designated Gemini 9A. Lovell and Aldrin were moved from being the backup crew of Gemini 10 to be the backup crew of Gemini 9. This cleared the way through the crew rotation for Lovell and Aldrin to become the prime crew of Gemini 12.\n</p><p>Along with the deaths of Grissom, White, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Chaffee" title="Roger Chaffee">Roger Chaffee</a> in the fire of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">Apollo 1</a>, this final arrangement helped determine the makeup of the first seven Apollo crews, and who would be in position for a chance to be the first to walk on the Moon.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Missions">Missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_5_control_room.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3157" data-file-width="4295" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Gemini_5_control_room.jpg/220px-Gemini_5_control_room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Gemini_5_control_room.jpg/330px-Gemini_5_control_room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Gemini_5_control_room.jpg/440px-Gemini_5_control_room.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_5_control_room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gemini Mission Control in Houston during Gemini 5</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1964 and 1965 two Gemini missions were flown without crews to test out systems and the heat shield. These were followed by ten flights with crews in 1965 and 1966. All were launched by Titan II launch vehicles. Some highlights from the Gemini program:\n</p>\n<ul><li>On <a href="/wiki/Gemini_4" title="Gemini 4">Gemini 4</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ed_White_(astronaut)" title="Ed White (astronaut)">Ed White</a> became the first American to make an <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA, or "space walk") on June 3, 1965.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gemini_5" title="Gemini 5">Gemini 5</a> (August 21-29, 1965) demonstrated the 8-day endurance necessary for an Apollo lunar mission with the first use of <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a> to generate its electrical power.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gemini_6A" title="Gemini 6A">Gemini 6A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gemini_7" title="Gemini 7">7</a> accomplished the first <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> in December 1965, and Gemini 7 set a 14-day endurance record.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gemini_8" title="Gemini 8">Gemini 8</a> achieved the first <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">space docking</a> with an unmanned Agena Target Vehicle.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gemini_10" title="Gemini 10">Gemini 10</a> established that radiation at high altitude was not a problem, further demonstrated the ability to rendezvous with a passive object, and would also be the first Gemini mission to fire the Agena\'s own rocket. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Mike Collins</a> would be the first person to meet another spacecraft in orbit, during his second successful EVA.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gemini_11" title="Gemini 11">Gemini 11</a> set a manned Earth orbital altitude record of 739.2 nautical miles (1,369.0&#160;km) in September 1966, using the Agena target vehicle\'s propulsion system. This record still stands as of 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumoulin_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumoulin-26">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On <a href="/wiki/Gemini_12" title="Gemini 12">Gemini 12</a> <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Edwin "Buzz" Aldrin</a> became the first space traveller to prove that useful work could be done outside a spacecraft without life-threatening exhaustion, due to newly implemented footholds, handholds and scheduled rest periods.</li></ul>\n<p>Rendezvous in orbit is not a straightforward maneuver. Should a spacecraft increase its speed to catch up with another, the result is that it goes into a higher and slower orbit and the distance thereby increases. The right procedure is to go to a lower orbit first and which increases relative speed, and then approach the target spacecraft from below and decrease orbital speed to meet it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rendezAldrin_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-rendezAldrin-27">[22]</a></sup> To practice these maneuvers special rendezvous and docking simulators were built for the astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rendezSim_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-rendezSim-28">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 210px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1733" data-file-width="1729" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg/180px-Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg/269px-Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg/359px-Ed_White_with_Space_Gun_maneuvering_unit.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Edward White during spacewalk, Gemini 4, June 1965\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">\n<div class="thumb" Stafford">Stafford</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gene_Cernan" title="Gene Cernan">Cernan</a>\n</td>\n<td>3&#8211;6 June 1966\n</td>\n<td>13:39 UTC\n</td>\n<td>03d 00h 20m 50s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="7">Rescheduled from May to rendezvous and dock with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augmented_Target_Docking_Adapter" title="Augmented Target Docking Adapter">Augmented Target Docking Adapter</a> (ATDA) after the original Agena Target Vehicle launch failed. The ATDA shroud did not completely separate, making docking impossible. Three different types of rendezvous, two hours of EVA, and 44 orbits were completed.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Gemini_10" title="Gemini 10">Gemini X</a><br/> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ge10Patch_orig.png"><img alt="Ge10Patch orig.png" data-file-height="2330" data-file-width="2590" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ge10Patch_orig.png/45px-Ge10Patch_orig.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ge10Patch_orig.png/68px-Ge10Patch_orig.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ge10Patch_orig.png/90px-Ge10Patch_orig.png 2x" width="45"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>GLV-10 12565\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">Young</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Collins</a>\n</td>\n<td>18&#8211;21 July 1966\n</td>\n<td>22:20 UTC\n</td>\n<td>02d 22h 46m 39s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="7">First use of the Agena Target Vehicle\'s propulsion systems. The spacecraft also rendezvoused with the Agena Target Vehicle from Gemini VIII. Collins had 49 minutes of EVA standing in the hatch and 39 minutes of EVA to retrieve experiments from the Agena. 43 orbits completed.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Gemini_11" title="Gemini 11">Gemini XI</a><br/> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_11_patch.png"><img alt="Gemini 11 patch.png" data-file-height="6970" data-file-width="4872" decoding="async" height="64" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Gemini_11_patch.png/45px-Gemini_11_patch.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Gemini_11_patch.png/68px-Gemini_11_patch.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Gemini_11_patch.png/90px-Gemini_11_patch.png 2x" width="45"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>GLV-11 12566\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pete_Conrad" title="Pete Conrad">Conrad</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Gordon_Jr." title="Richard F. Gordon Jr.">Gordon</a>\n</td>\n<td>12&#8211;15 September 1966\n</td>\n<td>14:42 UTC\n</td>\n<td>02d 23h 17m 09s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="7">Gemini record altitude with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a> of 739.2 nautical miles (1,369.0&#160;km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumoulin_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dumoulin-26">[21]</a></sup> reached using the Agena Target Vehicle propulsion system after first orbit rendezvous and docking. Gordon made a 33-minute EVA and two-hour standup EVA. 44 orbits.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Gemini_12" title="Gemini 12">Gemini XII</a><br/> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_12_insignia.png"><img alt="Gemini 12 insignia.png" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="902" decoding="async" height="46" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gemini_12_insignia.png/45px-Gemini_12_insignia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gemini_12_insignia.png/68px-Gemini_12_insignia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gemini_12_insignia.png/90px-Gemini_12_insignia.png 2x" width="45"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>GLV-12 12567\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Lovell</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Aldrin</a>\n</td>\n<td>11&#8211;15 November 1966\n</td>\n<td>20:46 UTC\n</td>\n<td>03d 22h 34m 31s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="8">Final Gemini flight. Rendezvoused and docked manually with the target Agena and kept station with it during EVA. Aldrin set an EVA record of 5 hours and 30 minutes for one space walk and two stand-up exercises, and demonstrated solutions to previous EVA problems. 59 orbits completed\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gemini-Titan_launches_and_serial_numbers">Gemini-Titan launches and serial numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Gemini-Titan launches and serial numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Titan II GLV</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:398px;max-width:398px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gemini_Profiles.jpg"><img alt="All Gemini Launches from GT-1 through GT-12" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Gemini_Profiles.jpg/290px-Gemini_Profiles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Gemini_Profiles.jpg/435px-Gemini_Profiles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Gemini_Profiles.jpg/580px-Gemini_Profiles.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GPN-2000-000612.jpg"><img alt="USAF serial number location on Titan II" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="267" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/GPN-2000-000612.jpg/100px-GPN-2000-000612.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/GPN-2000-000612.jpg/150px-GPN-2000-000612.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/GPN-2000-000612.jpg/200px-GPN-2000-000612.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Left: All Gemini launches from GT-1 through GT-12. Right: USAF serial number location on Titan II</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Gemini-Titan II</a> launch vehicle was adapted by NASA from the U.S. Air Force <a href="/wiki/LGM-25C_Titan_II" title="LGM-25C Titan II">Titan II ICBM</a>. (Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_LV-3B" title="Atlas LV-3B">Mercury-Atlas</a> launch vehicle had been adapted from the USAF <a href="/wiki/SM-65_Atlas" title="SM-65 Atlas">Atlas missile</a>.) The Gemini-Titan II rockets were assigned Air Force serial numbers, which were painted in four places on each Titan II (on opposite sides on each of the first and second stages). USAF crews maintained Launch Complex 19 and prepared and launched all of the Gemini-Titan II launch vehicles. Data and experience operating the Titans was of value to both the U.S. Air Force and NASA.\n</p><p>The USAF serial numbers assigned to the Gemini-Titan launch vehicles are given in the tables above. Fifteen Titan IIs were ordered in 1962 so the serial is "62-12XXX", but only "12XXX" is painted on the Titan II. The order for the last three of the 15 launch vehicles was canceled on July 30, 1964, and they were never built. Serial numbers were, however, assigned to them prospectively: <i>12568</i> - GLV-13; <i>12569</i> - GLV-14; and <i>12570</i> - GLV-15.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_cost">Program cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Program cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>From 1962 to 1967, Gemini cost $1.3 billion in 1967 dollars ($7.32&#160;billion in 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-29">[24]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lafleur20100308_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-lafleur20100308-1">[1]</a></sup> In January 1969, a NASA report to the US Congress estimating the costs for Mercury, Gemini, and Apollo (through the first manned Moon landing) included $1.2834 billion for Gemini: $797.4 million for spacecraft, $409.8 million for launch vehicles, and $76.2 million for support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_location_of_hardware">Current location of hardware</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Current location of hardware">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span href="/wiki/Adler_Planetarium" title="Adler Planetarium">Adler Planetarium</a>, Chicago, Illinois</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trainers">Trainers</span><span Rocket Center">U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center</a>, Huntsville, Alabama</li>\n<li>MSC-307&#160;&#8212;<a href="/wiki/USS_Hornet_Museum" title="USS Hornet Museum">USS <i>Hornet</i> Museum</a>, former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAS_Alameda" title="NAS Alameda">NAS Alameda</a>, Alameda, California</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_extensions_and_applications">Proposed extensions and applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Proposed extensions and applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advanced_Gemini">Advanced Gemini</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Advanced Gemini">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Gemini" title="Advanced Gemini">Advanced Gemini</a></div>\n<p>McDonnell Aircraft, the main contractor for Mercury and Gemini, was also one of the original bidders on the prime contract for Apollo, but lost out to <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a>. McDonnell later sought to extend the Gemini program by proposing a derivative which could be used to fly a <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">cislunar</a> mission and even achieve a manned lunar landing earlier and at less cost than Apollo, but these proposals were rejected by NASA.\n</p><p>A range of applications were considered for Advanced Gemini missions, including military flights, space station crew and logistics delivery, and lunar flights. The Lunar proposals ranged from reusing the docking systems developed for the Agena Target Vehicle on more powerful upper stages such as the Centaur, which could propel the spacecraft to the Moon, to complete modifications of the Gemini to enable it to land on the lunar surface. Its applications would have ranged from manned lunar flybys before Apollo was ready, to providing emergency shelters or rescue for stranded Apollo crews, or even replacing the Apollo program.\n</p><p>Some of the Advanced Gemini proposals used "off-the-shelf" Gemini spacecraft, unmodified from the original program, while others featured modifications to allow the spacecraft to carry more crew, dock with space stations, visit the Moon, and perform other mission objectives. Other modifications considered included the addition of wings or a parasail to the spacecraft, in order to enable it to make a horizontal landing.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Gemini">Big Gemini</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Big Gemini">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Big_Gemini" title="Big Gemini">Big Gemini</a></div>\n<p>Big Gemini (or "Big G") was another proposal by McDonnell Douglas made in August 1969. It was intended to provide large-capacity, all-purpose access to space, including missions that ultimately used Apollo or the Space Shuttle.\n</p><p>The study was performed to generate a preliminary definition of a logistic spacecraft derived from Gemini that would be used to resupply an orbiting space station. Land-landing at a preselected site and refurbishment and reuse were design requirements. Two baseline spacecraft were defined: a nine-man minimum modification version of the Gemini B called Min-Mod Big G and a 12-man advanced concept, having the same exterior geometry but with new, state-of-the-art subsystems, called Advanced Big G.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Three launch vehicles-<a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(rocket_family)" title="Titan (rocket family)">Titan IIIM</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saturn_INT-20" title="Saturn INT-20">Saturn INT-20</a> (S-IC/S-IVB) were investigated for use with the spacecraft.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_applications">Military applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Military applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Air Force had an interest in the Gemini system, and decided to use its own modification of the spacecraft as the crew vehicle for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_Orbital_Laboratory" title="Manned Orbital Laboratory">Manned Orbital Laboratory</a>. To this end, the Gemini 2 spacecraft was refurbished and flown again atop a mockup of the MOL, sent into space by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_III" title="Titan III">Titan III</a>C. This was the first time a spacecraft went into space twice.\n</p><p>The USAF also had the notion of adapting the Gemini spacecraft for military applications, such as crude observation of the ground (no specialized reconnaissance camera could be carried) and practicing making rendezvous with suspicious satellites. This project was called <a href="/wiki/Blue_Gemini" title="Blue Gemini">Blue Gemini</a>. The USAF did not like the fact that Gemini would have to be recovered by the US Navy, so they intended for Blue Gemini eventually to use the airfoil and land on three skids, carried over from the original design of Gemini.\n</p><p>At first some within NASA welcomed sharing of the cost with the USAF, but it was later agreed that NASA was better off operating Gemini by itself. Blue Gemini was canceled in 1963 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Defense" title="U.S. Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">Robert McNamara</a>, who decided that the NASA Gemini flights could conduct necessary military experiments. MOL was canceled by Secretary of Defense <a href="/wiki/Melvin_Laird" title="Melvin Laird">Melvin Laird</a> in 1969, when it was determined that unmanned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy_satellite" title="Spy satellite">spy satellites</a> could perform the same functions much more cost-effectively.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_media">In media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li>Two Gemini capsules (codenamed "Jupiter" instead of "Gemini") are featured in the plot of the 1967 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Bond_in_film" title="James Bond in film">James Bond</a></i> film <i><a href="/wiki/You_Only_Live_Twice_(film)" title="You Only Live Twice (film)">You Only Live Twice</a></i>.</li>\n<li>A modified one-man Gemini capsule is used to send an astronaut (played by <i><a href="/wiki/James_Caan" title="James Caan">James Caan</a></i>) to the moon in the 1968 film <i><a href="/wiki/Countdown_(1968_film)" title="Countdown (1968 film)">Countdown</a></i>.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/RocketSunIcon.svg/28px-RocketSunIcon.svg.png" stamps">US space exploration history on US stamps</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The only Gemini spacecraft not launched by a Titan II was the reflight of <a href="/wiki/Gemini_2" title="Gemini 2">Gemini 2</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Manned_Orbiting_Laboratory" title="Manned Orbiting Laboratory">Manned Orbiting Laboratory</a> test in 1966, which used a <a href="/wiki/Titan_IIIC" title="Titan IIIC">Titan IIIC</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Gemini_3" title="Gemini 3">Gemini 3</a> used the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Control_Center" title="Mercury Control Center">Mercury Control Center</a> located at Cape Kennedy for flight control, as the new center was still in a test status. Gemini 4 was the first to be guided from Houston, with Mercury Control as a backup. From Gemini 5 through today, all flights are controlled from Houston.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The requirement for a touchdown on land using a paraglider was canceled in 1964.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">During the ten manned flights of the Gemini program, the Soviets made no manned flights, and despite achieving the first EVA, did no more EVAs until January 1969.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Armstrong retired from the US Navy in 1960.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Gemini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><img alt="" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="196" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/PD-icon.svg/12px-PD-icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/PD-icon.svg/18px-PD-icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/PD-icon.svg/24px-PD-icon.svg.png 2x" width="12"/>&#160;This article incorporates&#160;<a class="external text" href="http://www.jsc.nasa.gov/policies.html#Guidelines" rel="nofollow">public domain material</a> from websites or documents of the <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
